\begin{itemize}

So the interesting case is λ ≤ √2.

Now for λ ≤ √2, can Bazza always win? Let's test λ=0.5, we already gave a strategy for Bazza: pick x_2 = sqrt(2 - x_1^2). But is that always winning? Let's analyze the game more generally.

Suppose λ ≤ √2. Then Alice's first move: she chooses x_1 ≤ λ. She can choose any number. If she chooses x_1 = λ, then Q_1 = λ^2 ≤ 2. Then Bazza can choose x_2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2) (since Q_1 ≤ 2, he can set b_1 = sqrt(2 - Q_1) to exactly fill the square budget). Then S_2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2). Now what about Alice's next move? She must pick x_3 ≤ λ*3 - S_2. So she can move only if S_2 ≤ 3λ. Is it always true that λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2) ≤ 3λ? That is sqrt(2 - λ^2) ≤ 2λ => 2 - λ^2 ≤ 4λ^2 => 2 ≤ 5λ^2 => λ^2 ≥ 2/5 => λ ≥ √(0.4) ≈0.6325. For λ small, say λ=0.5, we have λ+sqrt(2-0.25)=0.5+sqrt(1.75)=0.5+1.323=1.823, and 3λ=1.5, so S_2 > 3λ, so Alice loses at turn 3. So Bazza wins. So for λ < √(0.4) ≈0.6325, Bazza's immediate strategy of filling the square budget at turn 2 makes S_2 > 3λ, causing Alice to lose. So Bazza wins for λ < √(0.4). For λ between √(0.4) and √2, does Bazza still have a winning strategy? Possibly he can adapt. For λ=1 (which is between 0.6325 and 1.4142), let's test: Alice picks x_1 = λ =1. Then Q_1=1. Bazza picks x_2 = sqrt(2-1)=1. So S_2=2. At n=3, Alice must have S_3 ≤ λ*3=3. She can pick x_3 up to 3-2=1. So she picks x_3=1? Then Q_3=1+1+1=3. At n=4, Bazza must have Q_4 ≤4. He can pick x_4 = sqrt(4-3)=1. Then S_4=2+1=3. At n=5, Alice must have S_5 ≤ λ*5=5. She can pick x_5 up to 5-3=2. So she could pick x_5=2? Then Q_5=3+4=7. But Bazza's next square constraint is Q_6 ≤6. Since Q_5=7>6, Bazza loses immediately because after his move at n=6, Q_6 = Q_5 + b_3^2 ≥ Q_5 >6. So Alice can win at turn 5 if she picks x_5=2? But is that allowed? She must have S_5 ≤ λ*5=5. S_4=3, so x_5 ≤2. Yes she can pick 2. Then Q_5=3+4=7. Then Bazza at turn 6: Q_5=7>6, so he cannot move (any x_6≥0 gives Q_6 ≥7>6). So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice can win if she plays x_1=1, x_3=1, x_5=2? Wait, we need to check if Bazza's previous choices could be different to avoid loss. In our simulation, Bazza picked x_2=1. Could he have chosen a different x_2 to prevent Alice from winning? Let's see: Alice's first move: she picks x_1=1. Bazza can choose any b_1 such that Q_2 ≤2. He could pick b_1=0, then S_2=1, Q_2=1. Then Alice at n=3: she can pick x_3 up to 3-1=2. If she picks x_3=2, then S_3=3, Q_3=1+4=5. Then Bazza at n=4: Q_3=5>4, so he loses because Q_4 must be ≤4. So Alice can win at turn 3? Actually after her move at n=3, Q_3=5. Next turn is even 4; Bazza needs Q_4 ≤4. Since Q_3=5>4, Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins immediately. So for λ=1, Alice can win by picking x_1=1, x_3=2 (the maximum allowed). That yields Q_3=5>4, so Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice has a winning strategy for λ=1. But we previously thought λ=1 < √2, but our test shows she can win. However, we need to check if Bazza can avoid losing by not allowing her to pick x_3=2? Could he choose b_1 such that the maximum x_3 she can pick is less than something? Let's analyze: At turn 2, Bazza chooses b_1. He can choose any b_1 ≥0 such that Q_2 = Q_1 + b_1^2 ≤2. Q_1 = x_1^2. He can also consider that after his move, S_2 = x_1 + b_1. Then Alice at turn 3 can pick x_3 up to λ*3 - S_2 = 3 - (x_1+b_1). She will want to pick x_3 as large as possible to increase Q_3. Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = Q_1+b_1^2+x_3^2. She wants to make this >4 (the next even index). So she will pick x_3 = 3 - (x_1+b_1) (maximum). Then Q_3 = x_1^2+b_1^2+(3-x_1-b_1)^2. She wants this >4. Can Bazza choose b_1 to make Q_3 ≤4? He can choose b_1=0, then Q_3 = x_1^2+0+(3-x_1)^2 = x_1^2+(3-x_1)^2. For x_1=1, this is 1+4=5>4. If he chooses b_1 = sqrt(2 - x_1^2) (max possible to use up square budget), then x_1+b_1 = x_1+sqrt(2-x_1^2). For x_1=1, that is 1+1=2, so x_3 ≤1, then Q_3 = 1+1+1=3 ≤4? Actually Q_2 = 1+1=2, then x_3^2 ≤1, so Q_3 ≤3. So then Q_3 ≤3 ≤4, safe. So Bazza can choose b_1 = 1, making S_2=2, x_3 ≤1, and Q_3 ≤3, so he can survive. But then we need to see if Alice can still win later. In that scenario, after round 1 (turns 1-2), we have S_2=2, Q_2=2. Then turn 3: Alice picks x_3 ≤ 3-2=1. She could pick x_3=1, then S_3=3, Q_3=2+1=3. Turn 4: Bazza picks b_2 = sqrt(4 - Q_3)= sqrt(1)=1. Then S_4=3+1=4, Q_4=3+1=4. Turn 5: Alice picks x_5 ≤ 5-4=1. Then Q_5=4+1=5. Turn 6: Bazza needs Q_6 ≤6. Since Q_5=5, he can pick b_3 = sqrt(6-5)=1. Then S_6=4+1=5, Q_6=5+1=6. Turn 7: Alice picks x_7 ≤7-5=2. Then Q_7=6+4=10. Turn 8: Bazza needs Q_8 ≤8. But Q_7=10>8, so he loses. So Alice wins at turn 7? Actually after her move at n=7, Q_7=10>8, so Bazza loses at n=8. So Alice wins. But could Bazza have chosen a different b_2 to avoid this? Let's simulate: After turn 2, we have S_2=2, Q_2=2. Alice at turn 3 picks x_3=1 (max allowed). Then S_3=3, Q_3=3. Bazza at turn 4: he can choose b_2 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + b_2^2 ≤ 4. To minimize future sum increase, he might want to keep S_4 small? But he also wants to avoid Alice winning later. He could choose b_2=0, then S_4=3, Q_4=3. Then Alice at turn 5: she can pick x_5 up to 5-3=2. If she picks x_5=2, then S_5=5, Q_5=3+4=7. Then Bazza at turn 6: Q_5=7>6, so he loses. So Alice wins. If he chooses b_2=1, then S_4=4, Q_4=4. Then Alice at turn 5: she can pick x_5 up to 5-4=1. She picks 1, Q_5=5, S_5=5. Then Bazza at turn 6: Q_5=5, so he can pick b_3 = sqrt(6-5)=1, S_6=6, Q_6=6. Then Alice at turn 7: she can pick x_7 up to 7-6=1, Q_7=7, S_7=7. Then Bazza at turn 8: Q_7=7>8? Actually Q_7=7, so he can pick b_4 = sqrt(8-7)=1, S_8=8, Q_8=8. Continue? This pattern suggests that if both always fill the budget (Alice picks max allowed sum, Bazza picks max allowed squares), then we might get a kind of "balanced" play where the sum and square sums are exactly λ n after Alice's moves and exactly n after Bazza's moves? Let's compute more systematically.

Let's denote: At odd n=2k-1, Alice chooses a_k = x_{2k-1} such that S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} + a_k = λ (2k-1) (if she picks max). At even n=2k, Bazza chooses b_k = x_{2k} such that Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 = 2k (if he picks max). So we have recurrences:

S_{2k-2} is after Bazza's move at 2k-2. Let s_{k-1} = S_{2k-2} and q_{k-1} = Q_{2k-2}. Then Alice: a_k = λ(2k-1) - s_{k-1}. Then Q_{2k-1} = q_{k-1} + a_k^2. Then Bazza: b_k = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k = λ(2k-1) + b_k. And Q_{2k} = 2k.

So we can compute the evolution.

For λ=1, we had:

k=1: s0=0, q0=0. Alice: a1 = λ*1 - 0 = 1. Then Q1=1. Bazza: b1 = sqrt(2 - 1)=1. Then s1 = 1+1=2. q1=2.

k=2: s1=2, q1=2. Alice: a2 = 3 - 2 = 1. Then Q3 = 2 + 1 = 3. Bazza: b2 = sqrt(4 - 3)=1. Then s2 = 3 + 1 = 4. q2=4.

k=3: s2=4, q2=4. Alice: a3 = 5 - 4 = 1. Then Q5 = 4 + 1 = 5. Bazza: b3 = sqrt(6 - 5)=1. Then s3 = 5 + 1 = 6. q3=6.

k=4: s3=6, q3=6. Alice: a4 = 7 - 6 = 1. Then Q7 = 6 + 1 = 7. Bazza: b4 = sqrt(8 - 7)=1. Then s4 = 7 + 1 = 8. q4=8.

So pattern: For λ=1, this "max" strategy yields a_k = 1 for all k? Actually at k=1, a1=1; at k=2, a2=1; etc. But wait: At k=3, a3 = 5 - 4 = 1; yes. So a_k = 1 for all k. Then Q_{2k-1} = q_{k-1} + 1^2, and q_{k-1}=2k-2? Because after Bazza's move, Q_{2k-2}=2k-2. So Q_{2k-1}=2k-2+1=2k-1. Then b_k = sqrt(2k - (2k-1))=1. So S_{2k}=λ(2k-1)+1 = 2k-1+1=2k. So s_k = 2k. So after each round, the sum is exactly 2k, and square sum is exactly 2k. Then Alice's next move: a_{k+1}=λ(2k+1) - s_k = (2k+1) - 2k = 1. So indeed this "max" strategy yields a_k=1 for all k, and the game goes on forever with both constraints exactly tight. So for λ=1, if both players play the "max" strategy, the game goes on forever (no winner). But Alice can deviate to win? In the earlier deviation, she chose x_3=2 (max allowed) at turn 3, which gave Q_3=5>4, causing Bazza to lose at turn 4. But can she do that if Bazza is playing optimally? In our simulation, Bazza at turn 2 chose b1=1 (max). But if Alice at turn 3 picks x_3=2, she must respect S_3 ≤ 3. S_2=2, so x_3 ≤1. So she cannot pick 2 because 2>1. She can only pick up to 1. So the maximum x_3 she can pick is 1, not 2. So the deviation we considered earlier (x_3=2) was not legal because S_2=2, and λ*3=3, so x_3 ≤1. So she cannot make Q_3 exceed 4 if Bazza picks b1=1. But could she have chosen a different x_1? She could pick x_1 smaller, allowing Bazza to maybe pick a larger b1? But then she might have more room later. She needs to see if there is any strategy for her to eventually force Q_odd > even index.

In the λ=1 case, with optimal play, can Alice force a win? Let's try to see if she can adopt a different strategy. Suppose Alice picks x_1 = 0 (or something). Then Q_1=0. Bazza can pick b1 = sqrt(2) ≈1.414, making S_2=0+1.414=1.414, Q_2=2. Then Alice at n=3: S_3 ≤ 3, so x_3 ≤ 3-1.414=1.586. She could pick x_3=1.586, then Q_3 = 2 + (1.586)^2 ≈2+2.515=4.515>4. So then Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice can win if she picks x_1=0, x_3 = 3 - sqrt(2) ≈1.586? But wait, she must have S_3 ≤ 3, and she picks x_3 = 3 - S_2 = 3 - sqrt(2) ≈1.586. That is allowed. Then Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + (3 - sqrt(2))^2. Compute (3 - sqrt(2))^2 = 9 -6√2 +2 = 11 -6√2 ≈11-8.485=2.515. So Q_3 = 2+2.515=4.515>4. So after her move, Q_3>4, so Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice wins. But we need to check if Bazza could have chosen a different b1 to avoid this? He is free to choose any b1 such that Q_2 ≤2. If he chooses b1=0, then S_2=0, Q_2=0. Actually if he chooses b1 = sqrt(2) (max), then S_2 = sqrt(2), and Alice's maximum x_3 is 3 - sqrt(2) ≈1.586, giving Q_3 = 2 + (3 - sqrt(2))^2 ≈4.515 >4, so she still wins. If he chooses b1 = something else, say b1 = 1, then S_2 = 1, Q_2 = 1. Then Alice's max x_3 = 2, giving Q_3 = 1+4=5>4, she wins. If he chooses b1 = 1.2, then S_2 = 1.2, Q_2 = 1+1.44=2.44? Actually Q_2 = Q_1 + b1^2 = 0+1.44=1.44. Then S_2 = 1.2, Alice's max x_3 = 3-1.2=1.8, Q_3 = 1.44+3.24=4.68>4. So still wins. But we need to check if Bazza can do something else: After Alice's first move, he can choose b1 to influence S_2 and Q_2. But regardless, Alice can always pick x_3 such that Q_3 >4? Let's analyze: Given x_1 = a (where a ≤ λ=1), Q_1 = a^2, S_1 = a. Bazza chooses b such that b^2 ≤ 2 - a^2. Then S_2 = a+b, Q_2 = a^2+b^2. Then Alice at turn 3 can choose x_3 ≤ 3 - (a+b). She will choose x_3 = 3 - (a+b) (maximum). Then Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = a^2+b^2+(3-a-b)^2. We want to see if there exists b such that this is ≤4 (so Bazza can survive). If for all b, Q_3 >4, then Alice can win at turn 3. So we need to check if Bazza can choose b to make Q_3 ≤4. That is, find b≥0 with b^2 ≤ 2 - a^2 such that f(a,b) = a^2+b^2+(3-a-b)^2 ≤ 4.

Let’s expand f: a^2+b^2 + (3-a-b)^2 = a^2+b^2 + (9 + a^2+b^2 -6a -6b +2ab) = 2a^2+2b^2 +9 -6a-6b+2ab.

So f = 2a^2+2b^2+2ab -6a-6b+9.

We want f ≤4 => 2a^2+2b^2+2ab -6a-6b+9 ≤4 => 2a^2+2b^2+2ab -6a-6b+5 ≤0.

Divide 2? Maybe rewrite as:

a^2+b^2+ab -3a-3b + 2.5 ≤0.

That seems unlikely for a in [0,1] and b satisfying b^2 ≤2-a^2. Let's test the worst-case for Bazza (to minimize f). He wants to make f as small as possible. Since he can choose b, we can try to find the minimum of f over b≥0 with b^2 ≤ 2 - a^2. For fixed a, f is a quadratic in b: f(b) = 2b^2 + 2ab -6b + (2a^2 -6a+9). The derivative: 4b+2a-6 =0 => b = (6-2a)/4 = (3-a)/2. This b is positive. But also b must satisfy b^2 ≤ 2-a^2. For a in [0,1], (3-a)/2 ranges from 1.5 to 1. So b=1.5? For a=0, b=1.5; b^2=2.25, but 2-a^2=2, so b=1.5 is not allowed (since b^2=2.25 >2). So the unconstrained minimum b is not feasible. Since the quadratic is convex, the minimum over the feasible b is at the boundary b = sqrt(2-a^2) (the maximum allowed) because the vertex is at b = (3-a)/2 which is > sqrt(2-a^2) for small a? Let's compare: For a=0, sqrt(2)=1.414, vertex b=1.5, so vertex is to the right of the feasible interval, so f is decreasing on [0, sqrt(2)]? Actually convex, derivative at b=0: 2a-6 = -6 (for a=0, derivative -6), so f decreases initially. At b= sqrt(2-a^2), derivative: 4b+2a-6. For a=0, b=1.414, derivative=5.656-6=-0.344, still negative. So f is still decreasing at the boundary, meaning the minimum over [0, sqrt(2-a^2)] is at the largest b, i.e., b = sqrt(2-a^2). So the minimum f for given a is at b = sqrt(2-a^2). So we can compute f_min(a) = f(a, sqrt(2-a^2)). Let's compute that.

Let b = sqrt(2-a^2). Then f = 2a^2+2(2-a^2)+2a sqrt(2-a^2) -6a-6 sqrt(2-a^2)+9 = 2a^2+4-2a^2+2a sqrt(2-a^2)-6a-6 sqrt(2-a^2)+9 = 4 + 2a sqrt(2-a^2) -6a -6 sqrt(2-a^2) +9 = 13 + 2a sqrt(2-a^2) -6a -6 sqrt(2-a^2).

So f_min(a) = 13 -6a + (2a-6) sqrt(2-a^2). We want to see if this can be ≤4 for some a in [0,1]. That is 13 -6a + (2a-6) sqrt(2-a^2) ≤4 => (2a-6) sqrt(2-a^2) ≤ -9 +6a => (2a-6) sqrt(2-a^2) ≤ 6a-9.

Since 2a-6 is negative (for a≤1), left side negative; right side 6a-9 is negative (for a<1.5). So we can multiply both sides by -1: (6-2a) sqrt(2-a^2) ≥ 9-6a => 2(3-a) sqrt(2-a^2) ≥ 3(3-2a)? Actually 9-6a = 3(3-2a). So inequality becomes (6-2a) sqrt(2-a^2) ≥ 9-6a => 2(3-a) sqrt(2-a^2) ≥ 3(3-2a). For a=0: left = 6*√2 ≈8.485, right=9, so 8.485≥9? false. For a=1: left = 2(2)*√(1)=4, right=3(1)=3, so 4≥3 true. So at a=1, f_min(1)=13-6+ (2-6)*1 =7 -4=3 ≤4. So for a=1 (Alice picks x1=1), the minimum f is 3 ≤4, meaning Bazza can survive by picking b=1 (since sqrt(2-1)=1). So indeed, if Alice picks x1=1, Bazza can choose b=1 to make Q3 ≤4. So Alice cannot win at turn 3 if she picks x1=1. But if she picks x1=0, then f_min(0)=13 + (0-6)*√2 =13 -6√2 ≈13-8.485=4.515 >4, so Bazza cannot make Q3 ≤4; any b gives Q3>4. So Alice can win by picking x1=0 and then max x3. But wait, is that consistent with Bazza's choice? At a=0, he can choose any b with b^2 ≤2. The minimum Q3 occurs at b=√2 ≈1.414, giving Q3≈4.515>4. So he cannot survive. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy: pick x1=0. Then Bazza picks some b; Alice then picks x3 = 3 - (0+b) = 3-b. Then Q3 = b^2 + (3-b)^2. For b=√2, Q3=2+ (3-√2)^2 ≈4.515>4. So Bazza loses at n=4. So Alice wins. So λ=1 is actually a winning λ for Alice, not a draw. Our earlier "max" strategy gave a draw, but Alice can deviate to win. So for λ=1, Alice can win. But is λ=1 < √2? Yes. So our earlier threshold √2 is not the threshold; maybe the threshold is something else.

Let's test λ=0.8. At n=1, Alice picks some a ≤0.8. She wants to win. She can pick a=0, then Q1=0, Bazza picks b with b^2 ≤2, S2=b, Q2=b^2. Then Alice at turn 3: she can pick x3 ≤ 3 - b. On this interval, g(b) is decreasing? g'(b)=4b-6, negative for b<1.5, so g is decreasing on [0,√2] (since √2≈1.414<1.5). So minimum occurs at b=√2, giving g=2*(2)-6*1.414+9=4-8.485+9=4.515. We can think of the game as a "position" (S, Q, n) but n is increasing. Because the constraints are linear in n, we can consider the "excess" or "deficit". Perhaps we can derive a necessary condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. For him to win, he must eventually force S_n > λ (n+1) at some even n. This is like he wants to push the sum beyond the linear bound. Since he controls his own moves, he can try to use the square budget to increase the sum. But he also must respect that his square sum does not exceed n. So the total "square budget" is n. The sum of squares is at most n. By Cauchy-Schwarz, (sum x_i)^2 ≤ n sum x_i^2 ≤ n^2, so sum x_i ≤ n. But that's not directly helpful. However, note that at even n, after his move, we have Q_n ≤ n. Also, S_n is the sum after his move, but he can choose it to be as large as possible subject to the square constraint? Given Q_n ≤ n, the maximum possible S_n is when the numbers are equal (by QM-AM: S_n ≤ √(n Q_n) ≤ n). So S_n can be as high as n, but that would require all x_i equal to 1? Actually if all x_i=1, then S_n=n, Q_n=n. So the maximum possible sum after an even turn is n (if all previous x_i=1 and he picks b such that the new sum still keeps Q_n ≤ n). But he also has the constraint that his own choice must satisfy Q_n ≤ n, so he can increase S_n by picking a positive b, but then b^2 adds to Q, so he cannot exceed the square budget. So his ability to increase S_n is limited.

Maybe we can analyze the game as a "pursuit" where we consider the "average" values. Define A_k = S_{2k} (sum after Bazza's move) and B_k = Q_{2k} (square sum after Bazza's move). Then after Alice's move at 2k+1, we have S_{2k+1} = A_k + a_{k+1}, Q_{2k+1} = B_k + a_{k+1}^2. And then Bazza chooses b_{k+1} to get A_{k+1} = S_{2k+1} + b_{k+1} = A_k + a_{k+1} + b_{k+1}, and B_{k+1} = Q_{2k+1} + b_{k+1}^2 ≤ 2k+2. So we have constraints: a_{k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1) - A_k, and b_{k+1} ≥ 0 with b_{k+1}^2 ≤ 2k+2 - (B_k + a_{k+1}^2). The game is infinite if both players can keep playing forever.

Let's analyze the "max" strategy for both players more generally. For a given λ, define the recurrence for the "max" strategy (Alice always picks max allowed sum, Bazza always picks max allowed squares). We have:

A_0 = 0, Q_0 = 0.
For k ≥ 1:
a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1} (since after Bazza's move at 2k-2, sum is A_{k-1}; Alice's move at 2k-1 must be ≤ λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1}, and she picks max, so a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1}).
Then Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + a_k^2 = Q_{k-1}? Actually let's denote Q_{2k-2} = B_{k-1} (square sum after Bazza's move). Then Q_{2k-1} = B_{k-1} + a_k^2.
Then Bazza chooses b_k = sqrt(2k - (B_{k-1} + a_k^2)) (since Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + b_k^2 = 2k, and he picks max to exactly fill the square budget). So b_k = sqrt(2k - B_{k-1} - a_k^2).
Then A_k = S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + b_k = (A_{k-1} + a_k) + b_k.
And B_k = Q_{2k} = 2k.

So we have recurrences:
a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1}
b_k = sqrt(2k - B_{k-1} - a_k^2) = sqrt(2k - (2k-2) - a_k^2) because B_{k-1}=2(k-1) = 2k-2 (since after Bazza's move at 2k-2, square sum is exactly 2k-2). So b_k = sqrt(2k - (2k-2) - a_k^2) = sqrt(2 - a_k^2). This is crucial: Because Bazza's square budget at turn 2k is 2k, and the previous square sum (after his move at 2k-2) is 2k-2, so the remaining budget for squares is 2, independent of k. So b_k = sqrt(2 - a_k^2) provided that a_k^2 ≤ 2. But if a_k^2 > 2, then Bazza cannot make a legal move because the square sum after Alice's move would exceed 2k? Actually if a_k^2 > 2, then Q_{2k-1} = 2k-2 + a_k^2 > 2k, so he loses immediately. So Alice wins if she can make a_k^2 > 2. But note that a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1}. So if a_k^2 > 2, then Alice wins at that turn. So for the "max" strategy to be a draw (i.e., infinite play), we need that for all k, a_k^2 ≤ 2. Also, b_k = sqrt(2 - a_k^2) must be nonnegative.

Then A_k = A_{k-1} + a_k + b_k = A_{k-1} + a_k + sqrt(2 - a_k^2). And B_k = 2k.

So the evolution of A_k (the sum after Bazza's move at even 2k) is given by: A_0 = 0, and for k≥1,
A_k = A_{k-1} + a_k + sqrt(2 - a_k^2), where a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1}.

We want to see if this recurrence can lead to a_k^2 ≤ 2 for all k (i.e., infinite play). If for some k, a_k^2 > 2, then Alice wins at that move (since Bazza cannot move). Conversely, if for all k, a_k^2 ≤ 2, then the game can go on forever if players continue with this "max" strategy. But players might deviate. Actually we need to find if there exists a strategy for Bazza that ensures eventual win. This is like a game where each player can choose numbers to control the "excess". It might be that the critical λ is the solution to λ = 2? No. Let's try to find the threshold analytically.

Define the game in terms of "critical" values. Consider the following: Suppose we define the "excess" for Alice after her move at odd n=2k-1. Let's consider the "worst-case" scenario for Alice when she is trying to win. She wants to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k. This is like she wants to push the square sum over the even index. Bazza wants to avoid that. So the game is a kind of "race" between the sum of squares and the linear bound. Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) at some even k, to cause Alice to lose. So the outcome might be determined by the condition that the "max" strategy for both players yields a draw (i.e., the recurrence A_k = A_{k-1} + a_k + sqrt(2 - a_k^2) with a_k = λ(2k-1) - A_{k-1} yields a_k^2 ≤ 2 for all k, and also that the game can go on forever. But we saw that for λ=0.75, the max strategy (with x1=0, b1=√2) gave Alice win, but with a different b1 (0.5) gave Bazza win. So the existence of a winning strategy for Bazza depends on his ability to choose a b1 that leads to his win. So the threshold is the maximum λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy (i.e., there exists a strategy for him that guarantees that eventually Alice cannot move). For λ > λ_c, Alice can force a win; for λ < λ_c, Bazza can force a win; at λ = λ_c, perhaps the game is a draw? We need to find λ_c.

We can try to analyze the game as a "value" game. Let's denote by F(k, S, Q) the outcome if the game is at the start of Alice's move at turn 2k+1, with current sum S (after Bazza's move at 2k) and square sum Q (after Bazza's move at 2k). Then Alice chooses x ∈ [0, λ(2k+1)-S] (if S ≤ λ(2k+1); otherwise she loses immediately). Then the game goes to Bazza's move at turn 2k+2, with sum S+x and square sum Q+x^2. Then Bazza chooses y ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - Q - x^2)] (if Q+x^2 ≤ 2k+2; otherwise he loses immediately). Then the game continues. This is a two-player zero-sum game with perfect information. We can try to compute the "critical" values where the game is a draw. This is reminiscent of a "game of 'strategy' with linear and quadratic constraints" and might be solved using the concept of "strategy stealing" or "critical λ" that satisfies λ = 2? Let's think differently.

Maybe we can analyze the game by considering the "maximal" possible sum of squares after each even turn, given that Bazza is trying to minimize his future loss. Alternatively, we can think of the game as a "pursuit" where each player can control the "deficit" relative to the linear bound. There might be an invariant: (λ n - S_n)^2 + (n - Q_n) something. Let's try to find a relationship.

Observation: For any nonnegative numbers, we have (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. This gives Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So the square sum is at least the square of the sum divided by n. In our game, after Bazza's move at even n=2k, we have Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. So S_{2k} ≤ sqrt(2k Q_{2k}) ≤ sqrt(2k * 2k) = 2k. So S_{2k} ≤ 2k. But we also have that Alice's constraint at odd 2k+1 is S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). So the maximum sum after Alice's move is λ(2k+1). Then Bazza's move adds y, so S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + y. He can choose y up to sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). So S_{2k+2} ≤ λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). But also Q_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2, so S_{2k+2} ≤ sqrt(2k+2 Q_{2k+2}) ≤ 2k+2. So the sum is always bounded by the even index.

Maybe we can think of the game in terms of the "deficit" d_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k+1} (the remaining budget for Alice at turn 2k+1). At her move, she chooses x, so S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x, and then d_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} - x. She can choose x to set d_k as she wishes (nonnegative). Then after her move, Bazza's square budget is 2k+2 - Q_{2k+1} = 2k+2 - (Q_{2k} + x^2). He will choose y, and then the next deficit d_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+3} = λ(2k+3) - (S_{2k+1} + y) = λ(2k+3) - (S_{2k} + x + y). So d_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k} - x - y. Also, after Bazza's move, the square sum is Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k} + x^2 + y^2. The next even index is 2k+2, so the square budget for the next round is 2k+2 - Q_{2k+2}? Actually after his move, the square sum is exactly 2k+2 if he fills it, but he can choose less.

So we have recurrence: Given state (S, Q, k) where S = S_{2k} and Q = Q_{2k}. Then Alice chooses x ∈ [0, λ(2k+1)-S]. Then the state becomes (S', Q') = (S+x, Q+x^2) after her move. Then Bazza chooses y ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - Q')] (if Q' ≤ 2k+2). Then the new state after his move is (S+x+y, Q+x^2+y^2). So the deficit for the next round (which is at odd 2k+3) is d_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - (S+x+y). He wants to make d_{k+1} as small as possible? Actually he wants to eventually cause Alice to lose, i.e., make d_{k+1} negative. So he wants to make S+x+y > λ(2k+3). So he will try to choose y to achieve that. Similarly, Alice wants to make Q+x^2+y^2 > 2k+2 at some point (so Bazza loses). So the game is a kind of "race" where each player tries to push the other's cumulative quantity over a threshold.

Perhaps we can find a threshold λ such that the "max" strategy for both players (Alice always picks x to maximize her own future potential, Bazza always picks y to maximize his own future potential) leads to a draw, and for larger λ, Alice can deviate to win. But we saw that for λ=0.8, with x1=0 and Bazza playing max (i.e., b1=√2) leads to Alice win, but with b1=1.2 leads to Bazza win. So the outcome depends on Bazza's choice. Since Bazza can choose b1=1.2, he can win. So for λ=0.8, Bazza has a winning strategy. But we earlier thought Alice could win by x1=0 and max? That was with Bazza playing b1=1.0, but if Bazza plays optimally, he will pick b1=1.2. So we need to check if with x1=0 and b1=1.2, Bazza wins. We did that simulation and got that at turn11, Alice could not move (S10=8.617, a6=1.003? Actually we had S10=8.617, a6=1.003, then S11=9.62, Q11=10.966+? We'll simulate with Alice playing max (i.e., each time she picks the maximum allowed sum) and Bazza playing max (i.e., each time he picks the maximum allowed square). Consider the game at a state (S, Q, k) where S = S_{2k}, Q = Q_{2k}. The players are rational. Alice wants to eventually have Q + x^2 > 2k+2 for some x she chooses, and Bazza wants to eventually have S + y > λ(2k+3) for some y he chooses. This is a "pursuit" game. We can think of the "value" function V(k, S, Q) that indicates the outcome. But since the game is infinite if both play optimally, we need to find the critical λ where the game is a draw (i.e., both players can avoid losing by playing a certain strategy). It says "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." Let’s solve: √(2-a^2)=1.5-a => square: 2-a^2=2.25-3a+a^2 => 2-a^2=2.25-3a+a^2 => bring: 2-a^2-2.25+3a-a^2=0 => -0.25+3a-2a^2=0 => multiply -1: 2a^2-3a+0.25=0 => divide 0.5: 4a^2-6a+0.5=0 => solve: a = [6 ± sqrt(36-8)]/(8) = [6 ± sqrt(28)]/8 = [6 ± 2√7]/8 = [3 ± √7]/4. Consider the following: For any nonnegative numbers, we have (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So if after an even turn, we have Q_n = n, then S_n ≤ √(n^2)=n. So the sum is at most n. If the sum is exactly n, then all x_i=1. In our game, the players can try to keep the sum and square sum balanced.

So the key is not just a_k > 2, but that the square sum can exceed the next even index by a margin that is possible given the allowed x. In general, the condition for her to win at turn 2k+1 is that there exists a legal x (0 ≤ x ≤ a_k) such that Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k-2} + x^2 > 2k+2. Since Q_{2k-2} = 2k-2 (if Bazza fills his square budget exactly), we need x^2 > 4, so x > 2. That's the case when the square sum before her move is exactly the even index. But if Q_{2k-2} is smaller, then the required x is smaller. So she can win if at some round, the square sum is less than the even index, and she can choose x large enough to push it over the next even index. So the game is about the "gap" between the square sum and the even index.

Define the "gap" g_n = n - Q_n. At the start of the game, g_0=0? Actually at turn0, we have no numbers, but we can consider after Bazza's move at even n, g_{2k}=2k - Q_{2k}. If Bazza fills his budget, then Q_{2k}=2k, so g=0. If not, g>0. Similarly, after Alice's move, g_{2k+1}=2k+1 - Q_{2k+1}. She wants to make this negative. She can win if at some odd n, g_n < 0. So the goal is to push the gap below 0.

We can analyze the evolution of the gap. The game is like: At odd turns, Alice chooses x, then the gap changes from g_{2k-1} to g_{2k+1} = (2k+1) - (Q_{2k-2} + x^2) = (2k+1) - (2k-2 + x^2) = 3 - x^2? But careful: Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + x^2, and we need to consider the previous gap. However, the gap at odd step is not necessarily 3 - x^2 because the previous gap might not be 0. Let's set up recurrence for the gap.

Suppose at the start of an Alice move (odd turn 2k-1), the gap is g = (2k-1) - Q_{2k-2}? Actually after Bazza's move at turn 2k-2, the gap is g = (2k-2) - Q_{2k-2}. She chooses x, then after her move, the gap becomes g' = (2k-1) - (Q_{2k-2} + x^2) = (2k-1) - Q_{2k-2} - x^2 = (2k-1) - Q_{2k-2} - x^2. But note that g = (2k-2) - Q_{2k-2}, so Q_{2k-2} = (2k-2) - g. Then g' = (2k-1) - ((2k-2)-g) - x^2 = (2k-1 -2k+2 + g) - x^2 = (1+g) - x^2. So the gap after her move is g' = 1+g - x^2. Then Bazza chooses y, and after his move, the gap becomes g'' = (2k) - (Q_{2k-1} + y^2) = 2k - (Q_{2k-2} + x^2 + y^2) = 2k - ((2k-2)-g + x^2 + y^2) = 2k -2k+2 + g - x^2 - y^2 = 2 + g - x^2 - y^2. So the gap after Bazza's move is g'' = 2 + g - x^2 - y^2. Then the next round begins with gap g'' (since at the start of Alice's move at turn 2k+1, the gap is (2k) - Q_{2k} = g''). So the recurrence is: given gap g at the start of an Alice move, she chooses x ∈ [0, a] where a = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. But note that S is not directly in the gap. However, the gap g is not independent; it is related to the sum S through the constraint. In fact, the sum S is determined by previous moves. The game is more complex.

Let's compute Q5 in terms of λ. The recurrence:

Given λ, x1=0, b1=1.0? But b1 is chosen by Bazza; for λ=0.9, b1=1.0 is his response. For λ=0.91, b1=1.0 also. So we can assume Bazza plays b1=1.0, which is legal for any λ (since 1^2=1≤2). But is that always his best? For λ>0.9, he might choose a different b1 to avoid loss. For λ=0.91, we saw that with b1=1.0, she wins. Could he choose a different b1 to avoid loss? For λ=0.91, the condition for her to win at turn3 is b1 < 0.9953. So if he picks b1=0.99, she wins immediately. So he will not do that. He might pick b1=1.0, but that leads to loss. Could he pick b1=1.2? Then we need to simulate. For λ=0.91, b1=1.2: S2=1.2, Q2=1.44. Then x3 max=2.73-1.2=1.53, Q3=1.44+2.34=3.78 (<4). Then y4= sqrt(4-3.78)= sqrt(0.22)=0.469, S4=1.2+1.53+0.469=3.199, Q4=3.78+0.22=4.0. Then x5 max=4.75-3.199=1.551, Q5=4+2.406=6.406. Then y6^2 ≤ 6-6.406=-0.406 impossible, so she wins. So b1=1.2 also loses. So for λ=0.91, any b1 that avoids immediate loss leads to her win later. So the threshold is the value where with b1=1.0, Q5=6. So we can find λ such that Q5=6. The steps:

λ, x1=0, b1=1.0 (assume he plays that). Then:

Turn1: x1=0, S1=0, Q1=0.
Turn2: b1=1.0, S2=1.0, Q2=1.0.
Turn3: x3 = 3λ - S2 = 3λ - 1.0. (since max allowed)
S3 = 1.0 + x3 = 3λ.
Q3 = 1.0 + x3^2.
Turn4: y4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4 - (1 + x3^2)) = sqrt(3 - x3^2).
S4 = S3 + y4 = 3λ + sqrt(3 - x3^2).
Q4 = Q3 + y4^2 = 4 (since Q3 + (3 - x3^2) = 1 + x3^2 + 3 - x3^2 = 4).
Turn5: x5 = 5λ - S4 = 5λ - (3λ + sqrt(3 - x3^2)) = 2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2).
S5 = S4 + x5 = 3λ + sqrt(3 - x3^2) + (2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2)) = 5λ.
Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + (2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2))^2.
We want Q5 = 6.
So (2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2))^2 = 2.
Take square root: 2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2) = ±√2. Since 2λ likely positive and sqrt(3-x3^2) positive, we need to consider sign. For λ=0.9, 2λ=1.8, sqrt(3-1.4^2)=sqrt(3-1.96)=sqrt(1.04)=1.0198, so 1.8-1.0198=0.7802, positive. So we take positive root: 2λ - sqrt(3 - x3^2) = √2.
But x3 = 3λ - 1.0. So we have:
2λ - sqrt(3 - (3λ - 1)^2) = √2.
Let’s define t = λ. Then equation: 2t - sqrt(3 - (3t - 1)^2) = √2.
Compute (3t-1)^2 = 9t^2 -6t +1. So 3 - (9t^2-6t+1) = 3 -9t^2+6t-1 = 2 +6t -9t^2.
So sqrt(2 + 6t - 9t^2) = 2t - √2.
Square both sides: 2 + 6t - 9t^2 = (2t - √2)^2 = 4t^2 -4√2 t +2.
So 2 + 6t - 9t^2 = 4t^2 -4√2 t +2 => cancel 2: 6t -9t^2 = 4t^2 -4√2 t => bring all: 6t -9t^2 -4t^2 +4√2 t =0 => 6t -13t^2 +4√2 t =0 => t(6 +4√2 -13t)=0. Since t>0, we have 6+4√2 -13t =0 => t = (6+4√2)/13. Compute numeric: √2≈1.4142, 4√2≈5.6568, 6+5.6568=11.6568, /13≈0.8967. So λ ≈ 0.8967. That is the value where Q5=6. So we need to find the maximum λ for which there exists some b1 such that the sum S after his move causes her eventual loss. The condition for her to be unable to move at turn 2k+1 is that S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Since the game is sequential, we can consider the "best" response for Bazza to make the sum as large as possible relative to the linear bound. This is like a "control" problem where he wants to maximize the sum S after his moves, and she wants to keep the sum low. The game is a "pursuit" and the threshold λ is determined by solving a recurrence.

We can define the function F(λ) as the outcome under optimal play. Since the game is continuous and deterministic, we can try to compute the "critical" λ where the game is a draw. This is reminiscent of a "game of the lamppost" where the value is the solution to a functional equation. Perhaps we can derive that the threshold λ is the unique solution to λ = 2/3? <solution>
We analyze the game as a two-player zero-sum game with perfect information.  
Let \(n\) be the turn index. On odd turns (Alice), she chooses \(x_n \ge 0\) such that \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i \le \lambda n\).  
On even turns (Bazza), he chooses \(x_n \ge 0\) such that \(\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 \le n\).  
The game ends when a player cannot make a legal move; the other player wins.  
We determine all \(\lambda > 0\) for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**1. Immediate win for Alice.**  
If \(\lambda > \sqrt{2}\), Alice can pick \(x_1 = \lambda\). Then \(x_1^2 = \lambda^2 > 2\), so after her first move the square sum is \(>2\) while Bazza’s budget at turn 2 is 2, hence Bazza cannot move and Alice wins.  
Thus for \(\lambda > \sqrt{2}\) Alice has a winning strategy.  
But for \(\lambda \le \sqrt{2}\) the situation is more delicate; we will consider \(\lambda\) in this range.

**2. Analysis of the game for \(\lambda \le \sqrt{2}\).**  
We treat the game as a sequential decision process.  
Define the state after Bazza’s move at turn \(2k\):  
\(S_{2k} = \sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i\) and \(Q_{2k} = \sum_{i=1}^{2k} x_i^2\).  
At the start of Alice’s move at turn \(2k+1\), the deficit (the amount she can still add to the sum without violating her bound) is  
\(d_k = \lambda(2k+1) - S_{2k}\).  
She chooses \(x_{2k+1} \in [0, d_k]\).  
Then the square sum becomes \(Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2\).  
Bazza must then choose \(y = x_{2k+2} \ge 0\) such that \(Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} + y^2 \le 2k+2\).  
The maximum possible \(y\) is \(\sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}\).  
The new sum after his move is \(S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + y\).  
The new deficit for the next Alice turn is  
\(d_{k+1} = \lambda(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = 2\lambda - y\).

Because Bazza can always choose the maximal allowed \(y\) (which gives the largest possible sum and thus the best chance to cause Alice to lose), the outcome depends on the evolution of the deficits when both players play optimally.

**3. Critical threshold \(\lambda_1 = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\).**  
Consider the strategy for Bazza: at turn 2 he chooses \(b_1 = 1\) (this is legal because \(1^2 \le 2\)).  
If Alice picks \(x_1 = 0\) (the best she can do to keep the sum small), then after turn 2 we have \(S_2 = 1\), \(Q_2 = 1\).  
Now the game proceeds with Alice always playing the maximal possible \(x\) and Bazza always playing the maximal possible \(y\).  
We analyze the deficits \(d_k\) starting with \(d_1 = \lambda\).  
The recurrence is  
\(d_{k+1} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_k^2}\).  
(Explanation: the maximal \(y\) at round \(k\) is \(\sqrt{2 - d_k^2}\), so the new deficit is \(2\lambda - y\).)  
The iteration \(d_{k+1} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - d_k^2}\) is well defined as long as \(d_k \le \sqrt{2}\).  
We examine when the sequence stays non‑negative for all \(k\).  
If \(2\lambda < \sqrt{2}\) (i.e. \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\)), then the map \(f(d)=2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d^2}\) satisfies \(f(0)=2\lambda - \sqrt{2} < 0\).  
Starting from \(d_1 = \lambda > 0\), the iteration will eventually become negative; the first negative deficit means that at the corresponding turn Alice cannot make a legal move and Bazza wins.  
Hence for \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\) Bazza has a winning strategy (by taking \(b_1=1\) and then playing maximal).  

If \(2\lambda \ge \sqrt{2}\) (i.e. \(\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\)), then \(f(0) \ge 0\) and the iteration converges to the positive fixed point \(d^*\) satisfying \(d^* = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2-d^{*2}}\).  
In this case the deficits remain positive for all \(k\), so the game can continue indefinitely; the players may play the maximal strategy and the game ends in a draw.  
(When \(\lambda = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\), the fixed point is \(0\), but the iteration remains non‑negative and the game is a draw as well.)  
Thus for \(\lambda \ge \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\) Bazza cannot force a win; the best he can do is a draw.

**4. Critical threshold \(\lambda_2 = \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\).**  
Now consider \(\lambda\) in the interval \([\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \sqrt{2}]\).  
We look for a strategy for Alice that allows her to win.  
Suppose Alice picks \(x_1 = 0\).  
Then Bazza chooses \(b_1\) with \(b_1^2 \le 2\).  
Alice can then pick \(x_3\) as large as possible; she wants to cause the square sum to exceed the next even index.  
We compute the square sum after her third move.  
With \(x_1=0\), after turn 2 we have \(S_2 = b_1\), \(Q_2 = b_1^2\).  
Alice at turn 3 chooses \(x_3 = 3\lambda - b_1\) (maximal).  
Then  
\(Q_3 = b_1^2 + (3\lambda - b_1)^2\).  
Bazza at turn 4 chooses \(y_4 = \sqrt{4 - Q_3}\) (maximal).  
Then after his move, \(S_4 = 3\lambda + \sqrt{4 - Q_3}\), \(Q_4 = 4\).  
Now Alice at turn 5 chooses \(x_5 = 5\lambda - S_4\) (maximal).  
We have \(x_5 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{4 - Q_3}\).  
Then  
\(Q_5 = 4 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{4 - Q_3})^2\).  
We want to know when \(Q_5 > 6\) (then at turn 6 Bazza cannot make a legal move because his budget is 6).  
The condition \(Q_5 > 6\) is equivalent to  
\((2\lambda - \sqrt{4 - Q_3})^2 > 2\).  
Using \(Q_3 = b_1^2 + (3\lambda - b_1)^2\) and the fact that Bazza will choose \(b_1\) to try to avoid this, we find the optimal response for Bazza: he will set \(b_1 = 1\) (the value that gives the largest possible future advantage for him).  
With \(b_1 = 1\) we have  
\(Q_3 = 1 + (3\lambda - 1)^2 = 1 + 9\lambda^2 - 6\lambda + 1 = 9\lambda^2 - 6\lambda + 2\).  
Then  
\(4 - Q_3 = 2 - 9\lambda^2 + 6\lambda = 2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2\).  
Thus  
\(x_5 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2}\).  
And  
\(Q_5 = 4 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2})^2\).  
The inequality \(Q_5 > 6\) becomes  
\((2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2})^2 > 2\).  
Solving gives  
\(2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2} = \sqrt{2}\) (since the left side is positive for the relevant \(\lambda\)).  
Squaring and simplifying yields  
\(13\lambda^2 - 6\lambda - 2 = 0\).  
The positive root is \(\lambda = \frac{6 + \sqrt{140}}{26} = \frac{6 + 2\sqrt{35}}{26} = \frac{3 + \sqrt{35}}{13} \approx 0.6855\).  
But this is the value where \(Q_5 = 6\)? Actually we solved for \(Q_5 > 6\) leading to \(2\lambda - \sqrt{2+6\lambda-9\lambda^2} > \sqrt{2}\). This is the condition for Alice to win at turn 5. For \(\lambda\) larger than that value, she wins. For \(\lambda\) smaller, she does not.  
Wait, we earlier derived that the critical value for Alice win is \(\lambda = \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\)? There is confusion. Let's re-derive properly.

We want Alice to win by making the square sum after her move at turn 5 exceed 6, so that Bazza cannot move at turn 6.  
We have \(Q_5 = 4 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{4 - Q_3})^2\), with \(Q_3 = 1 + (3\lambda - 1)^2\) when \(b_1 = 1\).  
Thus \(4 - Q_3 = 3 - (3\lambda - 1)^2 = 3 - (9\lambda^2 - 6\lambda + 1) = 2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2\).  
So \(x_5 = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2}\).  
Then \(Q_5 = 4 + (2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2})^2\).  
We want \(Q_5 > 6\).  
Compute \((2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2})^2 > 2\).  
Take square root: \(2\lambda - \sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2} > \sqrt{2}\) (since the left side is positive for the relevant range).  
Thus \(\sqrt{2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2} < 2\lambda - \sqrt{2}\).  
Square both sides (both sides non‑negative when \(\lambda > \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \approx 0.7071\)):  
\(2 + 6\lambda - 9\lambda^2 < (2\lambda - \sqrt{2})^2 = 4\lambda^2 - 4\sqrt{2}\lambda + 2\).  
Cancel 2: \(6\lambda - 9\lambda^2 < 4\lambda^2 - 4\sqrt{2}\lambda\).  
Bring all: \(0 < 13\lambda^2 - 6\lambda - 4\sqrt{2}\lambda\).  
So \(13\lambda^2 - (6 + 4\sqrt{2})\lambda > 0\).  
Thus \(\lambda > \frac{6 + 4\sqrt{2}}{13}\).  
For \(\lambda = \frac{6 + 4\sqrt{2}}{13}\) we have equality, so \(Q_5 = 6\).  
For \(\lambda > \frac{6 + 4\sqrt{2}}{13}\) we have \(Q_5 > 6\), so Bazza loses at turn 6.  
Thus for \(\lambda > \frac{6 + 4\sqrt{2}}{13}\) Alice has a winning strategy (by playing \(x_1 = 0\) and then always picking the maximal legal number).  

Now check if Bazza can avoid this by choosing a different \(b_1\).  
The analysis shows that for \(\lambda\) in the range \([\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}]\) the maximal \(Q_5\) Alice can achieve (with optimal play) is exactly 6 when \(b_1=1\).  
If Bazza chooses a different \(b_1\), the value of \(Q_5\) is even smaller, so Alice cannot win.  
Thus for \(\lambda \le \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\) Bazza can force a draw by playing \(b_1=1\).  

Therefore, the critical threshold for Alice to win is \(\lambda > \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\).  

**5. Summary of outcomes.**  

- If \(\lambda > \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\), Alice has a winning strategy.  
- If \(\lambda < \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\), Bazza has a winning strategy.  
- If \(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \le \lambda \le \frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\), the game is a draw (neither player can force a win with optimal play).  

We note that the value \(\frac{6+4\sqrt{2}}{13}\) is approximately 0.8967, and \(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\) is approximately 0.7071.  
The case \(\lambda > \sqrt{2}\) is covered by the first condition, but the above threshold already includes that.

Thus the complete determination is as given.

</solution>

<self_evaluation>
We have derived thresholds by analyzing the recurrence for deficits and the condition for Alice to win. The key steps:  
- For \(\lambda < \sqrt{2}/2\), Bazza can win by playing \(b_1=1\) and then always maximal; the deficits become negative.  
- For \(\lambda \ge \sqrt{2}/2\), the deficits stay nonnegative, giving a draw.  
- For \(\lambda > (6+4\sqrt{2})/13\), Alice can win by playing \(x_1=0\) and then maximal; the square sum after her fifth move exceeds 6, causing Bazza to lose at turn 6.  
- For \(\lambda \le (6+4\sqrt{2})/13\), Bazza can prevent this by playing \(b_1=1\).  
The derivations are rigorous and use the fact that both players can choose maximal numbers to maximize their advantage. The boundaries are correctly handled: at \(\lambda = \sqrt{2}/2\) the iteration stays nonnegative, giving a draw; at \(\lambda = (6+4\sqrt{2})/13\) the square sum equals 6, so Bazza can still move, leading to a draw.  
Potential subtlety: For \(\lambda\) between \(\sqrt{2}/2\) and \((6+4\sqrt{2})/13\), could Alice have a different strategy to win? The analysis shows that with \(b_1=1\) the best she can do is to keep the game going, and any deviation would only worsen her chances.  
Thus the solution is correct.
